The Khmer Rouge captured the city, forcing Chum Mey and others to evacuate to the countryside. His two-year-old son died during the journey. Output (a): The article discusses the history of theatres in London, particularly those associated with William Shakespeare. The Theatre, the first purpose-built drama venue in Britain since Roman times, was located in Shoreditch and opened in 1576. Shakespeare lived and worked there after moving from Warwickshire to London. The Curtain, another theatre associated with Shakespeare, was built nearby in 1577. The Globe Theatre, Shakespeare's theatre group's venue, was established in 1599 south of the Thames. The Rose, another theatre opened in 1587, is thought to have hosted some of Shakespeare's early plays. All of these theatres were eventually destroyed, either by Puritan distaste for theatre, the Great Fire of London, World War II bombing, or indifference to their fate. The Globe Theatre has been reconstructed, and theShaLT project has helped to uncover and preserve the remains of the other theatres.  Output (b): The article talks about the history of theatres in London, focusing on those associated with William Shakespeare. The Theatre, built in 1576, was the first purpose-built drama venue in Britain since Roman times and where Shakespeare lived and worked after moving to London. The Curtain, another theatre associated with Shakespeare, was built in 1577. The Globe Theatre, established in 1599, was the theatre group's venue south of the Thames. The Rose, opened in 1587, is believed to have hosted some of Shakespeare's early plays. All these theatres were eventually destroyed and are now either partially preserved or reconstructed. The detail about The Theatre being the first purpose-built home for drama in Britain since Roman times is only present in Output (a). 3. The mention of the reason for the destruction of the theatres is only present in Output (a). 4. # Output (a) The article discusses the history of theatres in London, particularly those associated with William Shakespeare. The Theatre, the first purpose-built drama venue in Britain since Roman times, was located in Shoreditch and opened in 1576. Shakespeare lived and worked there after moving from Warwickshire to London. The Curtain, another theatre built around the same time, was also located nearby. The Globe theatre, established by Shakespeare's theatre group, the Chamberlain's Men, in 1599, was located south of the river. The Rose theatre, opened in 1587, is more intact than the sites north of the Thames and is thought to have played host to Titus Andronicus. However, all of these theatres were destroyed over time, either by Puritans, the Great Fire of London, World War II bombing, or indifference to their fate.  # Output (b) The article provides a historical overview of London theatres associated with William Shakespeare, including The Theatre, The Curtain, The Globe, and The Rose. The Theatre, the first purpose-built drama venue in Britain since Roman times, opened in Shoredidge in 1576. Shakespeare lived and worked there after moving from Warwickshire to London. The Curtain, another theatre built around the same time, was also located nearby. The Globe theatre, established by Shakespeare's theatre group, the Chamberlain's Men, in 1599, was located south of the river. The Rose theatre, opened in 1587, is more intact than the sites north of the Thames and is thought to have played host to Titus Andronicus. Unfortunately, all of these theatres were destroyed over time, either by Puritans, the Great Fire of London, World War II bombing, or indifference to their fate.  Differences Identification: 1. Output (a) starts with a brief mention of the article's context (450 years since Shakespeare's birth), while Output (b) does not. 2. Output (a) refers to the Globe theatre as "the Chamberlain's Men's Globe theatre," while Output (b) simply calls it "The Globe theatre." 3. Output (a) uses "Shakespearean London Theatres" project for the first time in the summary, while Output (b) does not. 4. Output (b) explicitly states that The Rose is more intact than the sites north of the Thames, while Output (a) does not.  Explanation and Rationale: Both Output (a) and Output (b) provide a comprehensive summary of the theatres mentioned in the article. However, Output (b) is more concise and directly addresses the instruction by summarizing the information about the theatres without including any additional context or information about the article. Output (a), on the other hand, includes some extra information that is not directly related to the instruction, such as the article's context and the mention of the ShaLT project. Output (a): - The Theatre (1576): First purpose-built theatre in Britain since Roman times, located in Shoreditch. Believed to be where Romeo and Juliet was first performed. - The Curtain (1577): Built 100 yards south of The Theatre. Excavation work revealed foundations, and Hackney Council approved redevelopment including a permanent display. - Globe Theatre (1599): Shakespeare's theatre group established it south of the river. The original site was identified in 1980s and is now a car park with a plaque. The reconstructed Shakespeare's Globe is nearby. - The Rose (1587): Opened before the Globe, believed to have hosted Titus Andronicus. Foundations flooded in 1989 to preserve them.  All theatres were destroyed by Puritans in the 1640s, the Great Fire of 1666, World War II bombing, and general indifference.  Output (b): - The Theatre (1576): First purpose-built theatre in Britain since Roman times, located in Shoreditch. Believed to be where Romeo and Juliet was first performed. A new 235-seat theatre is proposed as part of the site's redevelopment. - The Curtain (1577): Built 100 yards south of The Theatre. Excavation work revealed foundations, and Hackney Council approved redevelopment including a permanent display. - Globe Theatre (1599): Shakespeare's theatre group established it south of the river. The original site was identified in 1980s and is now a car park with a plaque. The reconstructed Shakespeare's Globe is nearby. - The Rose (1587): Opened before the Globe, believed to have hosted Titus Andronicus. Foundations flooded in 1989 to preserve them. Output (b): - 1576: The Theatre was built in Shoreditch, marking the first purpose-built home for drama in Britain since Roman times. - 1587: The Rose theatre opened, preceding the Globe. - 1599: Shakespeare's theatre group, the Chamberlain's Men, established the Globe theatre in Southwark. - 1640s: The theatres were demolished due to Puritan disapproval of theatre. - 1666: The Great Fire of London led to further destruction of the theatres. - World War II bombing caused additional damage to the sites. In his review of the murder of Pat Finucane, Sir Christopher de Silva adopted a "working definition" of collusion less broad than that of Judge Cory and more in line with that of the Billy Wright inquiry panel. early Americans consumed a significant amount of alcohol, with an average of 5.8 gallons of pure alcohol consumed per person per year in 1790. This consumption peaked at 7.1 gallons per year in 1830, and by the late 19th century, alcoholism was being treated as a disease. Prohibition was introduced in 1917, and by 1930, it was widely believed that making alcohol legal again would provide much needed jobs and taxes during the Great Depression. In 1933, the 21st Amendment ended Prohibition, and since then, alcohol consumption has decreased to an average of 2.3 gallons of pure alcohol per person per year.  Differences Identification: 1. .  # Output (a): The book Born in Lockdown, created by a writing project set up by Bristol novelist Emylia Hall, is a collection of writings from 277 new mothers in 2020. The mothers share their experiences of becoming a new mother during the pandemic, which was characterized by isolation, uncertainty, and pressure on mental health. However, they also found silver linings and positives in the shared experience. The writings were in fragments of up to 50 words, capturing raw, affecting, and inspiring moments and emotions. The book is a remarkable record of new motherhood during the pandemic, with resilience, love, and hope shining through.  Final Decision: Therefore, Output (b) is better. .  # Output (a): The book Born in Lockdown, created by a writing project set up by Bristol novelist Emylia Hall, is a collection of writings from 277 new mothers in 2020. The mothers share their experiences of becoming a new mother during the pandemic, which was characterized by isolation, uncertainty, and pressure on mental health. However, they also found silver linings and positives in the shared experience. The writings were in fragments of up to 50 words, capturing raw, affecting, and inspiring moments and emotions. The book is a remarkable record of new motherhood during the pandemic, with resilience, love, and hope shining through.  Final Decision: Therefore, Output (b) is better. January. VAT is now being collected at the point of sale rather than at the point of importation. This essentially means that overseas retailers sending goods to the UK are expected to register for UK VAT and account for it to HMRC if the sale value is less than €150 (£135).  Some retailers have temporarily closed their stores from UK-based customers until their UK VAT registration and the overall process of selling to the UK post-Therefore, Output (b) is better.
on sales of goods already in the UK at the point of sale, raising an anticipated £300m every year. "Many EU businesses which currently sell goods to UK customers will have already registered for UK VAT under existing rules and HMRC is working very closely with those who haven't to ensure they can comply with the changes."  Tackling evasion Adam French, Which? consumer rights expert, said consumers would be "frustrated" if the new VAT rules were applied in a way that causes significant inconvenience. "It's vital the government makes it clear to consumers as well as traders what the changes as a result of Brexit mean for them and how they shop. It should also work to ensure UK consumers can retain access to a wide range of competitively priced and quality goods."  Campaigner Richard Allen, founder of Retailers Against VAT Abuse Schemes, told the BBC that the massive increase in international online shopping had led to VAT evasion on a huge scale. He said the new HMRC rules were aimed at tackling that, but it was unclear how firms who failed to register for UK VAT would be dealt with.  "Why should a phonograph spares manufacturer in Idaho bother to register for VAT in theUK and how are you going to make them do it?" he said. "And if they send the package anyway, what are you going to do?" 'Not what we wanted' "For providing this service, [HMRC] intend to charge a fee to every company in the world in every country in the world which exports to the UK," said Dutch Bike Bits on its website. "Clearly this is ludicrous for one country, but imagine if every country in the world had the same idea. "If every country decided to behave in the same way, then we would have to pay 195 fees every year, keep up with the changes in taxation law for 195 different countries, keep accounts on behalf of 195 different countries and submit payments to 195 tax offices in 195 different countries, and jump through whatever hoops were required to prove that we were doing all of this honestly and without any error." Dutch Bike Bits said it had many customers within the UK and would like to be able to trade with them.
